## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: materials science / constitutive modeling
**Analysis**: This task models the stress-strain-temperature relationship of a material, a core problem in constitutive modeling. Stress typically increases with strain (elastic modulus, strain hardening) and decreases with temperature (thermal softening), with various classical models capturing these effects.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Hooke's law (linear elastic): sigma = c0 * epsilon (linear stress-strain at fixed T)
- Temperature-dependent elastic modulus: sigma = c0 * epsilon * (c1 - T) (linear elasticity with linearly decreasing modulus)
- Power-law hardening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 (plastic strain hardening, Hollomon equation)
- Ramberg-Osgood style: sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon**c2 (elastic + plastic combined)
- Johnson-Cook strength (isothermal, no rate): sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * (c3 - c4 * T**c5) (strain hardening with thermal softening)
- Arrhenius thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(c2 * T) (thermally activated softening with power-law hardening)
- Exponential thermal factor: sigma = c0 * epsilon * exp(c1 * T) (elastic modulus with exponential temperature dependence)
- Voce hardening with thermal softening: sigma = (c0 - c1 * exp(c2 * epsilon)) * (c3 - c4 * T) (saturating hardening with linear softening)
- Ludwik model: sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2 (yield stress plus power-law hardening)
- Temperature-dependent Ludwik: sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * exp(c3 * T) (Ludwik with Arrhenius-type thermal factor)
- Homologous temperature softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * (c2 - T)**c3 (power-law softening near melting point)
- Swift hardening: sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1)**c2 (shifted power-law to avoid zero-strain singularity)
- Temperature-dependent Swift: sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1)**c2 * (c3 - c4 * T) (Swift with linear thermal softening)
- Hyperbolic tangent saturation: sigma = c0 * tanh(c1 * epsilon) * (c2 + c3 * T) (saturating stress with temperature dependence)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Strain hardening saturation → Use (1 - exp(-c*epsilon)) or tanh(c*epsilon) to model stress approaching a saturation plateau at large strains
- Thermal softening as multiplicative factor → Temperature effects often multiply the strain-dependent term: sigma = f(epsilon) * g(T), reflecting independent mechanisms
- Homologous temperature scaling → Softening near melting uses (Tm - T)**c form, where stress vanishes at the melting temperature
- Yield stress offset → An additive constant c0 in sigma = c0 + f(epsilon) represents the initial yield stress before hardening
- Shifted strain origin → Using (epsilon + c0)**c1 instead of epsilon**c1 avoids singularities and models pre-strain or initial elastic contribution
- Arrhenius-type thermal activation → exp(c0 * T) or exp(c0 / T) captures thermally activated dislocation motion or diffusion-controlled deformation
- Polynomial thermal modulus → Elastic modulus varying as (c0 + c1*T + c2*T**2) captures non-linear temperature dependence of stiffness
- Strain-temperature cross term → epsilon*T interaction terms capture temperature-dependent hardening rates

## Analysis

The Ground Truth expression is:
```
61.35*epsilon*(-0.0508*(T - 278.15) + 1) + 98.58*epsilon^0.318*exp(-49.46/(8.314*T)) + 2.304*epsilon^1.45*(T - 278.15)
```

Let me expand and analyze the GT structure:

**Term 1:** `61.35*epsilon*(-0.0508*T + 0.0508*278.15 + 1)` = `61.35*epsilon*(−0.0508*T + 15.126)` = `−3.114*T*epsilon + 928.1*epsilon`

**Term 2:** `98.58*epsilon^0.318*exp(-5.949/T)` — This involves `epsilon^0.318 * exp(-c/T)`, a highly nonlinear term combining a fractional power of epsilon with an Arrhenius-type exponential in T.

**Term 3:** `2.304*epsilon^1.45*(T - 278.15)` = `2.304*T*epsilon^1.45 - 640.8*epsilon^1.45`

Key structural features of the GT:
1. **T*epsilon** term (linear in both T and epsilon)
2. **epsilon** term (linear in epsilon only)
3. **epsilon^0.318 * exp(-c/T)** — fractional power of epsilon times Arrhenius function of T
4. **T * epsilon^1.45** — T times fractional power of epsilon
5. **epsilon^1.45** — fractional power of epsilon

The critical features are:
- The **exp(-c/T)** Arrhenius dependence on T
- **Fractional powers of epsilon** (0.318 and 1.45)
- **Product of T with fractional power of epsilon** (T*epsilon^1.45)

Now examining the candidates:

- Most candidates use combinations of `T*epsilon`, `sin(...)`, `log(...)`, `epsilon/(c+epsilon)`, and similar terms.
- None of the candidates contain `exp(-c/T)` or `exp(c/T)` — the Arrhenius temperature dependence. Some have `exp(c*epsilon)` or `exp(T*c)`, but not `exp(1/T)`.
- None have `T*epsilon^c` with a free fractional exponent combined with the Arrhenius term.
- Candidates with `epsilon**c` (like #1, #3, #5, #6, #8, #11, #15) have `log(c*epsilon^c + 1)` or `epsilon^c` but lack the `exp(-c/T)` coupling.

The Arrhenius term `exp(-c/T)` is fundamentally different from any function of T present in the candidates (which are all linear in T, or have `exp(c*T)`, `exp(c*epsilon)`, `sin(T*c)`, etc.). No candidate can reproduce `exp(-c/T)` behavior.

Additionally, the `T*epsilon^1.45` term requires a product of T with a fractional power of epsilon, which most candidates cannot produce either (they typically have `T*epsilon` at most, or `T*exp(c*epsilon)`).

No candidate can match the GT's functional form.

Conclusion: No match

## Ground Truth Analysis

The GT expression is:
```
61.35*epsilon*(-0.05077*(T - 278.15) + 1) + 98.58*epsilon^0.3178*exp(-49.46/(8.314*T)) + 2.304*epsilon^1.450*(T - 278.15)
```

Let me expand the first term:
```
61.35*epsilon*(-0.05077*T + 0.05077*278.15 + 1) + 98.58*epsilon^0.3178*exp(-5.949/T) + 2.304*epsilon^1.450*(T - 278.15)
```

So the GT has these basis function components:
1. `epsilon*T` term (linear in both)
2. `epsilon` term (linear in epsilon only)
3. `epsilon^0.3178 * exp(-5.949/T)` - a fractional power of epsilon times a Boltzmann-like exponential in T
4. `epsilon^1.450 * T` - a fractional power of epsilon times T
5. `epsilon^1.450` - a fractional power of epsilon (constant in T)

The key non-standard basis functions are:
- **epsilon^0.3178 * exp(-c/T)**: Arrhenius-type term
- **epsilon^1.450**: fractional power of epsilon

These are very specific functional forms. Let me check each candidate for the presence of these basis functions.

## Key Observations

The GT contains:
- `exp(-c/T)` (Arrhenius term) multiplied by `epsilon^0.3178`
- `epsilon^1.450` (fractional power)
- `epsilon*T` (cross term)
- `epsilon` (linear)

Most candidates use combinations of: `T`, `epsilon`, `sin(...)`, `log(...)`, `epsilon/(c+epsilon)`, `exp(...)`, `1/(c+epsilon)`. None of these standard basis functions can reproduce `exp(-c/T)` or `epsilon^1.450` exactly.

Let me verify this systematically. The function `exp(-c/T)` cannot be represented by any polynomial, rational, trigonometric, or logarithmic function of T. Similarly, `epsilon^c` for non-integer/non-simple-fraction c cannot be represented by `log(epsilon)`, `sin(epsilon)`, `epsilon/(c+epsilon)`, etc.

Let me check if any candidate contains both `exp(something/T)` or `exp(c*T)` AND `epsilon^c` with free exponent:

Looking through all candidates:
- **Candidates with `epsilon**c` (free exponent)**: 1, 3, 5, 6, 8, 11, 15, 23, 28 (these have `epsilon**c` inside `log`)
- **Candidates with `exp(-c/T)` or similar**: None have `exp(c/T)`. Some have `exp(c*T)` (candidates 30, 31, 36) or `exp(c*epsilon)` (14, 19, 26, 29, 40, 47).

No candidate has `exp(-c/T)` which is fundamentally different from `exp(c*T)`. The Arrhenius term `exp(-c/T)` is a distinct basis function that cannot be matched by any combination of `T`, `exp(c*T)`, `log(T)`, `sin(T)`, polynomials in T, etc.

Since **no candidate contains `exp(-c/T)` or any equivalent form**, and this term has a significant coefficient (98.58), it cannot be zeroed out or absorbed into other terms. Therefore, **no candidate can match the GT**.

Let me verify there's no way to set the coefficient of the Arrhenius term to zero: c4 = 98.58 is fixed in the GT, so this term is always present and significant.
